We ablate the hyperparameters central to the design of BASED: (1) the choice of feature map  $\phi$  (see Section 4.1), (2) the size of the feature dim d' (we show the effective size of the feature after applying the feature map in parantheses, see Section 4.1), (3) the use of local sequence mixers (sliding window attention and short convolutions), and (4) the data-dependent decay defined in Section 4.

### <span id="page-35-1"></span>E. Experimental Details

### <span id="page-35-0"></span>E.1. Language Model Pretraining

We use A100 80GB Nvidia GPUs to run all experiments. We use training infrastructure closely adapted from the FlashAttention code base: https://github.com/Dao-AILab/flash-attention/tree/main for all pretraining runs (Dao, 2023). The Pile data is tokenized using the GPT2BPETokenizer and all models see the data in the same order. Here we provide details on the hyperaparamters and configurations used for training each architecture. We also provide details on the FLOPs computation.

- BASED We train using the specifications in Table 7. Our implementation is provided here: https://github.com/HazyResearch/based. The initial models were trained and evaluated using the Fast Transformer CUDA kernels discussed in Appendix B (Vyas et al., 2020; Katharopoulos et al., 2020b). We use no input-dependent decay whatsoever when training the models to 30b and 50b tokens at 360m and 1.3b parameters respectively.
- Transformer++ (Touvron et al., 2023) We refer to the modern Llama architecture with Rotary encodings, RMSNorm and SwiGLU as Transformer++, following prior work (Gu and Dao, 2023; Yang et al., 2023). We train using the the specifications in Table 8 using the Flash Attention training code provided here: https://github.com/Dao-AILab/flash-attention/tree/main (Dao, 2023).
- Mamba (Gu and Dao, 2023) We train using the specifications in Table 9, where the parameters are sourced from the Appendix of (Gu and Dao, 2023). The implementation is sourced from the provided reference at https://github.com/state-spaces/mamba.
- Hyena (Poli et al., 2023) We train using the specifications in Table 10, where the parameters are sourced from the Appendix of (Poli et al., 2023). The implementation is sourced from the provided reference at https://github.com/HazyResearch/safari.
- H3 (Fu et al., 2023a) We train using the specifications in Table 11. The implementation is sourced from the provided reference at https://github.com/HazyResearch/safari.
- RWKV (Peng et al., 2023) We train using the specifications in Table 12 and use the reference implementation at https://github.com/BlinkDL/RWKV-LM. We specifically evaluate RWKV-V5.
- Gated Linear Attention (GLA) We train using the specifications in Table 13. We train following the reference implementation at https://github.com/berlino/gated\_linear\_attention.

We give all models the improved Transformer++ recipe (e.g., SwiGLU) as relevant.

#### E.2. Computing Recurrent State Size

In this section, we provide details on how we compute the size of the recurrent hidden state for the results described in Section 3.1. We train and evaluate six sequence mixers on a synthetic associative recall task: attention (Vaswani et al., 2017), sliding window attention (Beltagy et al., 2020), Mamba (Gu and Dao, 2023), H3 (Fu et al., 2023a), Hyena (Poli et al., 2023), and BASED. For each, we vary hyperparameters that affect the memory consumption during inference. We compare how MQAR accuracy varies with the size of the recurrent hidden state.

**BASED.** The recurrent state size in BASED is determined by the model dimension d and the size of the hidden dimension after applying the feature map  $\tilde{d}$ . The +1 accounts for the K-state required for computing the denominator. For more details on the recurrent view of BASED, see 4.

$$\operatorname{sizeof}(s_i) = (d+1) \times \tilde{d} \tag{8}$$

In Based, we use the Taylor Exponential feature map after projecting d down to a smaller dimension d'. With this approach, recurrent state size is given by:

sizeof(
$$s_i$$
) =  $(d+1) \times (1 + \frac{3d'}{2} + \frac{d'^2}{2})$  (9)

In our synthetic experiments, we run BASED with  $d \in \{48, 64, 128\}$  and  $d' \in \{8, 16, 24\}$ .

Attention. The recurrent state size (*i.e.* KV-cache size) in attention depends on two parameters: the model dimension d and the sequence length N. The 2 in the expression below accounts for the separate storage for keys and values in the KV-cache.

$$\operatorname{sizeof}(\boldsymbol{s}_i) = 2 \times d \times N \tag{10}$$

In our synthetic experiments we run attention with d ∈ {64, 128}. The sequence length N is determined by the task, not the model architecture.

Sliding window attention. The recurrent state size in sliding window attention is given by the model dimension d and the width of the sliding window ksliding. The 2 in the expression below accounts for the separate storage for keys and values in the KV-cache.

$$\operatorname{sizeof}(\boldsymbol{s}_i) = 2 \times d \times \min(N, k_{\text{sliding}}) \tag{11}$$

In our synthetic experiment we run sliding window attention with d ∈ {128} and ksliding ∈ {8, 16, 32, 64, 128, 256, 512, 1024}.

Mamba. The recurrent state size in Mamba is determined by the model dimension d and the number of heads h. The 2 in the expression below accounts for the expansion in the Mamba block.

$$\operatorname{sizeof}(s_i) = 2 \times d \times d_{\operatorname{state}} \tag{12}$$

In our synthetic experiments, we run Mamba with d ∈ {64, 128, 256} and dstate ∈ {8, 16, 24}.

H3. The recurrent state size in H3 is determined by the model dimension d and the number of heads dstate.

$$\operatorname{sizeof}(\boldsymbol{s}_i) = d \times d_{\operatorname{state}} \tag{13}$$

In our synthetic experiments, we run H3 with d ∈ {64, 128, 256} and dstate = d 4 .

Hyena. The recurrent state size in Hyena is determined by the model dimension d and the number of heads h. The 2 in the expression below accounts for the separate storage for keys and values in the KV-cache.

$$\operatorname{sizeof}(s_i) = d \times N \tag{14}$$

In our synthetic experiments, we run Hyena with d ∈ {64, 128, 256}.

### E.3. Language Model Evaluation

In this section, we provide details on each of the evaluations (columns) reported in Tables [1](#page-7-0) and [6.](#page-34-0)

Pile *(Language Modeling).* First, we report overall perplexity on the Pile test set [\(Gao et al.,](#page-11-1) [2020\)](#page-11-1). Then , to understand how much of the perplexity gap is due to recall capacity, we also evaluate perplexity on two slices (*i.e.* subsets) of the test set:

- 1. *Associative recall(AR) tokens.* Tokens in the final position of a bigram which previously occured in context, but ≤ 1250 times in the training data.
- 2. *Other tokens.* All other tokens.

To construct these slices, we exactly follow the protocol in [Arora et al.](#page-9-0) [\(2023a\)](#page-9-0) and refer the reader to that work for more details. We compute these slices on the first 16 million tokens in the test set.

SWDE *(Information Extraction).* The task in the SWDE benchmark is to extract semi-structured relations from raw HTML websites. For example, given an IMBD page for a movie (*e.g. Harry Potter and the Sorcerer's Stone*) and a relation key (*e.g.* release date), the model must extract the correct relation value (*e.g.* 2001). The SWDE benchmark was originally curated by [Lockard et al.](#page-13-0) [\(2019\)](#page-13-0) for the task of open information extraction from the semi-structured web. Because we are evaluating the zero-shot capabilities of relatively small language models, we adapt the task to make it slightly easier. Our task setup is similar after to that used in [Arora et al.](#page-11-6) [\(2023b\)](#page-11-6).

FDA *(Information Extraction).* The task is to extract key-value pairs from a set of PDFs scraped from the FDA website. We use the dataset and labels collected in [\(Arora et al.,](#page-11-6) [2023b\)](#page-11-6). We break apart the documents into chunks of 1,920 tokens. For every key-value pair that appears in the chunk, we create a zero-shot prompt using the simple prompt template: {chunk} \n {key}:

We allow the model to generate a fixed number of tokens after the prompt and check (with case insensitivity) if the value is contained within the generation. We report accuracy, the fraction of prompts for which the generation contains the value.

Below we include one example of a zero-shot prompt for the key-value pair "*Type of Test: Quantitative, colorometric, pyranose oxidase (PROD)*". The actual chunk is substantially longer in the dataset (note the ellipsis).

510(k) SUBSTANTIAL EQUIVALENCE DETERMINATION DECISION SUMMARY ASSAY ONLY TEMPLATE A. 510(k) Number: k180209 B. Purpose for Submission: New Device C. Measurand: 1,5-Anhydroglucitol (1,5-AG) D. Type of Test: Quantitative, colorometric, pyranose oxidase (PROD) E. Applicant: Diazyme Laboratories Inc. F. Proprietary and Established Names: Diazyme 1,5-AG Assay G. Regulatory Information: 1. Regulation section: 21 CFR 864.7470; Glycosylated hemoglobin assay 2. Classification: Class II ... *[1,920 tokens of context from the PDF]* ... Diazyme's 1,5-AG assay uses the enzyme pyranose oxidase (PROD) to oxidize the 2nd position hydroxyl group of 1,5-AG and to detect the generated hydrogen peroxide by colorimetry using peroxidase (POD). Type of Test:

SQUAD *(Question Answering).* The Stanford Question Answering Dataset (SQUAD) can be used to evaluate the reading comprehension of language models. The model is given a passage of text and a question whose answer is contained in the passage.

Because the models trained in this work are relatively small-scale (up to 1.3 billion parameters trained on 10 billion tokens) and not instruction fine-tuned, they struggle to answer questions when asked directly. To make the task more amenable to these raw language models, we first use GPT-4 to reformat the questions to more closely resemble the next-token-prediction task the models were trained on:

```
Can you rewrite this question and answer as a statement. Ensure that the answer
is the last part of the statement. \n \n Question: {question} \n\n Answer:
{answer} \n\n Rewrite:
```

For example, the question and answer "*Question: Which NFL team represented the AFC at Super Bowl 50? Answer: Denver Broncos*" was rewritten by GPT-4 as "*The NFL team that represented the AFC at Super Bowl 50 was the Denver Broncos.*" We verify that the rewritten sentence does indeed end with the answer, discarding any sentences where it does not (40% of questions).

We run the reformatting on 5,000 squad questions from the validation set, yielding a final dataset of 2,984 questions formatted as next token predictions.

Below we include one example of a zero-shot prompt. The reformatted question is in bold.

For the third straight season, the number one seeds from both conferences met in the Super Bowl. The Carolina Panthers became one of only ten teams to have completed a regular season with only one loss, and one of only six teams to have acquired a 15–1 record, while the Denver Broncos became one of four teams to have made eight appearances in the Super Bowl. The Broncos made their second Super Bowl appearance in three years, having reached Super Bowl XLVIII, while the Panthers made their second Super Bowl appearance in franchise history, their other appearance being

#### Simple linear attention language models balance the recall-throughput tradeoff

Super Bowl XXXVIII. Coincidentally, both teams were coached by John Fox in their last Super Bowl appearance prior to Super Bowl 50. The team in Super Bowl 50 that had a 15-1 record was the

#### <span id="page-39-0"></span>F. Theoretical Results

#### F.1. Introduction

Our focus in this section will be on the theoretical results of the paper. Specifically, we will show the equivalence of models Based and Mamba (Gu and Dao, 2023) with BaseConv, a minimal gated-convolution operator (Arora et al., 2023a, Definition 4.1), and prove lower bounds for the MQAR problem (Arora et al., 2023a, Section H.7.1) in various settings. We begin by setting notation and introducing the theoretical formulations of the models.

**Notation.** We will be denoting the all 1 row vector of size k, given by  $\begin{bmatrix} 1 & 1 & \dots & 1 & 1 \end{bmatrix}$ , and the all 0 row vector of size k, given by  $\begin{bmatrix} 0 & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 \end{bmatrix}$ , as  $\mathbf{1}^k$  and  $\mathbf{0}^k$ , respectively. We will also construe the standard basis vector  $\mathbf{e}_i$  as a column vector in these notes, and adhere to the following matrix indexing convention:  $\mathbf{M}[i,j]$  is the entry in the ith row and the jth column,  $\mathbf{M}[i,:] \in \mathbb{F}^{1 \times n}$  denotes the ith row, and  $\mathbf{M}[:,j] \in \mathbb{F}^{m \times 1}$  denotes the jth column of  $\mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{F}^{m \times n}$ , where  $\mathbb{F}$  is a field and the reader can substitute  $\mathbb{F}$  for  $\mathbb{R}$  for convenience. For a matrix  $\mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times m}$ , we define the pair-wise Hadamard product of columns of  $\mathbf{M}$  as  $\mathbf{M} \circ \mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times m^2}$ , where

<span id="page-39-5"></span>
$$(\boldsymbol{M} \circ \boldsymbol{M})[:, i] := \boldsymbol{\mathbf{M}}[:, j] \odot \boldsymbol{\mathbf{M}}[:, k] \quad \text{for} \quad i \in [m^2],$$

$$j = \left\lfloor \frac{i-1}{m} \right\rfloor + 1, \quad k = (i-1) \mod m + 1.$$
(15)

Moreover, we define the element-wise exponentiation of a matrix  $\mathbf{M}$  as  $\exp[\mathbf{M}]$  where  $\exp[\mathbf{M}]_{ij} = \exp(\mathbf{M}_{ij})$ . Next, we denote the *Hadamard product* of vectors  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^n$  as  $\mathbf{u} \odot \mathbf{v}$ ; the operation can be extended to matrices accordingly, and for vectors  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^n$ , we denote their *linear (or acyclic) convolution* as  $\mathbf{u} * \mathbf{v}$ 

**Arithmetic Circuit Notation.** We briefly introduce the notation of arithmetic circuits (Bürgisser et al., 2013). An arithmetic circuit  $\mathcal{C}$  with variables  $X \triangleq \{x_1, x_2, \dots, x_n\}$  over a field  $\mathbb{F}$  is interpreted as a directed acyclic graph, where the input nodes are labelled by either the variables from X or constants from  $\mathbb{F}$  and the internal nodes are labelled by + or  $\times$  with the output being the polynomial computed at the output node.

We shall also refer to the *size* of the circuit as the number of nodes, the *depth* of the circuit as the length of the longest path between an input node and the output node, and the *width* of the circuit as the number of parallel operations in the circuit, or 'wires' which will be intersected by a horizontal 'cut' through the circuit. Moreover, the *degree* of a circuit is defined as the degree of the polynomial computed by the circuit. We summarize this with the following definition:

**Definition F.1.** An arithmetic circuit  $\mathcal{C}$  is an  $(n, s, \Delta, w)$ -circuit if  $\mathcal{C}$  is an n-variate arithmetic circuit of size s and of depth at most  $\Delta$ , and width w.

#### <span id="page-39-3"></span>F.2. The Models

We now introduce the definitions of the models Based and Mamba for the reader's convenience. Note that we have redefined these models to ensure consistency with the notation presented above.

#### F.2.1. BASED

The Based model combines two layer types: BaseConv and LinAtt defined below.

**Definition F.2** (BaseConv (Arora et al., 2023a)). Given an input sequence  $\boldsymbol{u} \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$ , where N is the sequence length and d is the model dimension, a learned weight matrix  $\boldsymbol{W}^B \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times d}$  and biases  $\boldsymbol{B}^B, \boldsymbol{B}^K \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$  and a matrix of convolution filters  $\boldsymbol{K} \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$ , a BaseConvlayer computes the following:

<span id="page-39-4"></span>
$$\boldsymbol{z}^{BaseConv} := (\boldsymbol{u}\boldsymbol{W}^B + \boldsymbol{B}^B) \odot (\boldsymbol{K} * \boldsymbol{u} + \boldsymbol{B}^K) \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}, \tag{16}$$

where the convolutions are applied across the input length N.

**Definition F.3** (LinearAttention (Katharopoulos et al., 2020b)). Given an input sequence  $u \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$ , where N is the sequence length and d is the model dimension, a set of linear projections Projection, Projection,  $\in \mathbb{R}^{d \times d'}$ , Projection,  $\in \mathbb{R}^{d \times d'}$ , where d' is the feature dimension, the LinearAttention layer computes the following:

<span id="page-39-2"></span>
$$z^{\text{LinearAttention}} := (\overline{Q} \, \overline{K}^{\top}) V \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d},$$
 (17)

<span id="page-39-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>6</sup>By linear projections of a matrix  $u \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}$ , we mean uW + B for some weight matrix  $W \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times n}$  and bias  $B \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}$ .

where Q := Projection<sup>q</sup> (u), K := Projectionk(u),V := Projection<sup>v</sup> (u), and we have

$$\begin{aligned} \overline{\bm{Q}} &= [\bm{1}, \bm{Q}, \bm{Q} \circ \bm{Q}] \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times (1 + d' + d'^2)}, \ \overline{\bm{K}} &= [\bm{1}, \bm{Q}, \bm{K} \circ \bm{K}] \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times (1 + d' + d'^2)}. \end{aligned}$$

### F.2.2. MAMBA

We now introduce the Mamba model from [\(Gu and Dao,](#page-9-4) [2023\)](#page-9-4).

<span id="page-40-4"></span>Definition F.4 (Mamba [\(Gu and Dao,](#page-9-4) [2023\)](#page-9-4)). Given an input sequence u ∈ R N×d , where N is the sequence length and d is the model dimension, the Mamba layer computes the following:

<span id="page-40-2"></span>
$$\boldsymbol{z}^{\text{Mamba}} := \text{SSM}(\overline{\boldsymbol{A}}, \overline{\boldsymbol{B}}, \boldsymbol{C})(\boldsymbol{u}) \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d},$$
 (18)

with the parameters, A ∈ R d×d , B ∈ R d , defined as

<span id="page-40-1"></span>
$$\overline{A} := \exp(\Delta A), 
\overline{B} := (\Delta A)^{-1} (\exp(\Delta A) - I) \cdot \Delta B, 
= A^{-1} (\exp(\Delta A) - I) \cdot B,$$
(19)

where d, the state dimension, and A ∈ R d×d are parameters of the model and do not depend on the input u, along with the following *input-dependent* parameters B, C ∈ R N×d , ∆ ∈ R <sup>N</sup>×<sup>d</sup> defined as

$$\begin{aligned} \bm{B} &:= \operatorname{Linear}_{N \times \overline{d}}(\bm{u}) \in \mathbb{R}^{\overline{d}}, \ \bm{C} &:= \operatorname{Linear}_{N \times \overline{d}}(\bm{u}) \in \mathbb{R}^{\overline{d}}, \ \Delta &:= \operatorname{Linear}_{N \times d}(\bm{u}) \in \mathbb{R} \end{aligned}$$

for i ∈ [N]. It is important to note here that the parameters B, C, ∆ are causal[7](#page-40-0) and we denote the dependence on upto the ith row of the input u for i ∈ [N] by adding a subscript i where the dependence for A<sup>i</sup> ∈ R d×d is inherited from ∆<sup>i</sup> in equation [19](#page-40-1) and we denote B[i, :] =: B<sup>i</sup> , C[i, :] =: C<sup>i</sup> .

Finally, the SSM in equation [18](#page-40-2) is realized as a linear recurrence. That is, for every (i, j) ∈ [N] × [d], we have

<span id="page-40-3"></span>
$$h[i,j] = \overline{A}_i h[i-1,j] + \overline{B}_i u[i,j]$$

$$z[i,j] = C_i^\top h[i,j]$$
(21)

where h[i, j] ∈ R d , z[i, j] ∈ R denote the latent state and the output of the SSM in Equation [\(18\)](#page-40-2), respectively.

### F.3. Equivalency to **BaseConv**

For a polynomial with variables X over a field F, there exists a corresponding arithmetic circuit C over X that computes the output of the polynomial at its terminating node when interpreted as a directed acyclic graph. For any such arithmetic circuit C of size s and depth ∆, [\(Arora et al.,](#page-9-0) [2023a,](#page-9-0) Theorem 4.2) showed the existence of an equivalent BaseConv operator that uses O˜ (s∆) parameters and O˜ (∆) layers. In the sequel, we use this result by expressing the model outputs computed in equation [17](#page-39-2) and equation [18](#page-40-2) as polynomials in u and exp(u) to show the equivalency between these disparate models. We would now like to recall [\(Arora et al.,](#page-9-0) [2023a,](#page-9-0) Theorem 4.2). Before doing so, we first establish the following definitions from [\(Arora et al.,](#page-9-0) [2023a\)](#page-9-0).

Definition F.5. An (︂ N, L, d, N˜ , d˜ )︂ − Gated Convolution Model is a stacked sequence to sequence model with L layers such that:

1. input and output are N × d matrices,

<span id="page-40-0"></span><sup>7</sup>That is, B[i, :], C[i, :] and ∆[i, :] depend only on u[0 · · ·i − 1].

- 2. each layer's operations consist of element-wise gating, convolution, linear projection, and
- 3. all the individual gated convolution layers take in  $\tilde{N} \times \tilde{d}$  matrices and output  $\tilde{N} \times \tilde{d}$  matrices. We refer to the tuple  $(\tilde{N}, \tilde{d})$  as the *inner dimension* of the model.

We also assume that the input  $u \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$  is embedded into  $u' \in \mathbb{R}^{\tilde{N} \times \tilde{d}}$  such that

$$u'[n, t] = \begin{cases} u[n, t] & \text{if } n < N, \ t < d \\ 0 & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

<span id="page-41-0"></span>The output from the last layer  $\boldsymbol{z} \in \mathbb{R}^{\tilde{N} \times \tilde{d}}$  is transformed into output  $\boldsymbol{y} \in R^{N \times d}$  by extracting the top left  $N \times d$  entries in  $\boldsymbol{z}$ . Theorem F.6 ((Arora et al., 2023a), Theorem 4.2). For any  $(nd, s, \Delta, w)$ -arithmetic circuit  $\mathcal{C}$ , there exists an equivalent  $\left(N, \Delta', d, \tilde{N}, \tilde{d}\right)$  — BaseConv with  $N = n, \Delta' = \mathcal{O}(\Delta \log w)$ ,  $\tilde{N} = \mathcal{O}(w)$ ,  $\tilde{d} = d$  that simulates  $\mathcal{C}$ .

Remark F.7. For notational simplicity, we will use  $u_{i,j}$  as the symbol for the variable in the polynomial in u representing the entry u[i,j].

We now present the results showing equivalency between the models in Appendix F.2 and the BaseConv layer in equation 16 using Theorem F.6.

<span id="page-41-1"></span>**Proposition F.8.** Given an input  $u \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$ , there exists an equivalent  $(N, O(\log^2(Nd)), d, O(N(d+d'^2), O(\max(d, d'^2)))$  – BaseConv that computes the output of the LinearAttention layer with feature dimension d', cf. Equation (17).

*Proof.* For the matrices  $Q, K \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d'}, V \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$  with the corresponding projection matrices  $W^Q, W^k \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times d'}, W^V \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times d}$ , a single BaseConv layer that computes each of these matrices by simply taking identical projection and  $h^s, h^l, B^s \equiv 0$  and  $B^\ell \equiv \mathbb{1}^{N \times d}$ , the all 1 matrix. Using the remembering primitive (Arora et al., 2023a, Proposition H.10), we can compute each of these in turn while remembering others using O(1) layers and Nd parameters.

Next, we derive an expression for each entry  $(i, j) \in [N] \times [d'^2]$  of  $\mathbf{Q} \circ \mathbf{Q}, \mathbf{K} \circ \mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d'^2}$ . From equation 15, observe that each entry of  $\mathbf{M} \circ \mathbf{M}$  can be written as the product of entries from  $\mathbf{M}$ . Hence we have

$$(\mathbf{Q} \circ \mathbf{Q})[i,j] \equiv \mathbf{Q}[i,k] \cdot \mathbf{Q}[i,\ell]$$

$$(\mathbf{K} \circ \mathbf{K})[i,j] \equiv \mathbf{K}[i,k] \cdot \mathbf{K}[i,\ell]$$
(22)

for  $k = \left\lfloor \frac{j-1}{d'} \right\rfloor + 1$ ,  $\ell = (j-1) \mod d' + 1$ . Note, however, that we can simulate the above by first increasing the inner dimension and copying over columns of  $\boldsymbol{Q}$  to get  $\boldsymbol{Q}_1, \boldsymbol{Q}_2 \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$  defined as  $\boldsymbol{Q}_1[i,j] := \boldsymbol{Q}[i,k]$  and  $\boldsymbol{Q}_2[i,j] := \boldsymbol{Q}[i,\ell]$  for  $k = \left\lfloor \frac{j-1}{d'} \right\rfloor + 1$ ,  $\ell = (j-1) \mod d' + 1$  so that  $(\boldsymbol{Q} \circ \boldsymbol{Q}) = \boldsymbol{Q}_1 \odot \boldsymbol{Q}_2$ , which, mutatis mutandis, also applies to  $(\boldsymbol{K} \circ \boldsymbol{K})$  We can achieve the copying of the columns by simply using the projection matrix  $\boldsymbol{W}^B$  and another permutation matrix  $\boldsymbol{P}$ . Apart from the multiplication by  $\boldsymbol{P}$ , we only need to use O(1) layers, and moreover, since the circuit that computes  $\boldsymbol{P}\boldsymbol{u}$  simply rearranges the input, there exists a single BaseConv layer that computes  $\boldsymbol{P}\boldsymbol{u}$  (Arora et al., 2023a, Corollary H.20). By the stacking lemma (Arora et al., 2023a, Lemma H.11), we can stack these layers to get a composition of the outputs so far to get a  $(N, O(1), d, O(N(d+d'^2), O(\max(d,d'^2))) - \text{BaseConv}$  model. Moreover, the concatenated matrices  $\overline{\boldsymbol{Q}}, \overline{\boldsymbol{K}} \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times (1+d'+d'^2)}$  then take the addition of the computed components so far which again takes O(1) layers of BaseConv.

Finally, we can express each entry  $(i,j) \in [N] \times [d]$  of the output of LinearAttention as a polynomial as follows:

$$\boldsymbol{z}_{i,j}(\boldsymbol{u}) \equiv \sum_{m \in [1+d'+d'^2], n \in [N]} \overline{\boldsymbol{Q}}[i,m] \cdot \overline{\boldsymbol{K}}[n,m] \cdot \boldsymbol{V}[n,j]. \tag{23}$$

Thus, we can derive the arithmetic circuit that computes  $\mathbf{z}_{i,j}(\mathbf{u})$  by taking in the outputs of the BaseConv layers so far as input and compute each of the terms inside the sum by multiplying the outputs from all three and compute the sum using additional  $\log \lceil Nd \rceil$  depth. Each term inside the sum requires two multiplication gates with depth 2, each of which serve as inputs to the circuit with size Nd computing the sum. Moreover, there are  $N \cdot d$  such output gates each of which is computed in parallel resulting in a circuit of size  $O(N \cdot d)$ , depth  $O(\log(Nd))$  and width O(Nd). O Overall, applying Theorem F.6 then results in an equivalent  $\left(N, O(\log^2(Nd)), d, O(N(d+d'^2), O(\max(d,d'^2))\right)$  — BaseConv model that computes  $\mathbf{z}$ .

#### F.4. The Lower Bounds

In the sequel, we consider the *multiple-query associative recall* problem (MQAR) as defined in (Arora et al., 2023a, Section H.7.1). We briefly recall the definition here.

Suppose we are given an input sequence  $u[0\cdots 3N-1] \triangleq \{(k_0, v_0, q_0), \dots, (k_{N-1}, v_{N-1}, q_{N-1})\}$  with each  $k_i, v_i, q_i \in C$  is a token drawn from a vocabulary of size c = |C|. Our goal is then to check, for each  $1 \leq i \leq N-1$ , whether there exists  $0 \leq j < i$  such that  $q_i \equiv k_j$ , and if so, output  $v_j$ .

#### F.4.1. THE SPACE COMPLEXITY OF AR

We will start by providing a lower bound on the space complexity of solving the standard associative recall (AR) problem. As AR is a subclass of MQAR, this naturally provides a lower bound on the space complexity of MQAR as well. Here, we formally recall the associative recall problem.

The AR problem takes key-value pairs  $\{k_i, v_i\}_{i=0}^{n-1}$  along with a query q appended at the end as input and the goal is to output  $v_i$  if  $q = k_i$  for some  $i \in [0, N-1]$ .

We now require a randomized communication complexity lower bound result for the *index problem*:

The index problem has two agents, Alice and Bob, where Alice has a string  $x \in \{0,1\}^n$  and Bob has an index  $i \in [n]$ , and the goal for the players is to output the *i*-th entry  $x_i$ . Moreover, we also require the communication to be *one-way*: only Alice is allowed to send a single message to Bob and Bob needs to output the answer.

We will make use of the following lower-bound result.

<span id="page-42-2"></span>**Theorem F.9** ((Jayram et al., 2008)). The one-way randomized communication complexity<sup>8</sup> of the index problem for sending an n-length bit string is  $\Omega(n)$ .

#### F.4.2. LOWER BOUND FOR RECURRENT MODELS

We now use Theorem F.9 to first provide a lower bound on the number of bits required by the following class of models to solve AR.

<span id="page-42-3"></span>**Definition F.10** (Recurrent Models). A model  $\mathcal{M}$  taking an input  $u \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$ , where N is the input length and d is the model dimension, is termed a *recurrent model* if its i-th state, representing the output at location i,  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathcal{M}}^{i} \in \mathbb{R}^{\tilde{d}}$ , with  $\tilde{d}$  denoting the state size, is determined exclusively by the preceding elements of the input  $u[0 \dots i-1]$ . The state  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathcal{M}}^{i}$  represents the accumulated information of the model depending on the inputs up to the i-th element, and is distinct from learned parameters that are static with respect to the input sequence.

Specifically,  $Z_{\mathcal{M}}^{i}(u) = \phi(u[0...i-1])$ , indicating that the state is a function of the input history but not of the entire input sequence simultaneously. Moreover, we can express this as:

$$\boldsymbol{Z}_{\mathcal{M}}^{i}(\boldsymbol{u}) = f_{\mathcal{M}}^{i}(\boldsymbol{Z}_{\mathcal{M}}^{i-1}, \boldsymbol{u}[i]), \tag{24}$$

for a sequence of functions  $\{f_{\mathcal{M}}^i\}_{i\in[N]}$ , where each function is tailored to evolve the state based on the immediate past state and the current input.

*Remark* F.11. Note that Definition F.10 excludes models that inherently require the entire input sequence for computation at any state, such as those based on non-causal convolutional operations over the full input.

<span id="page-42-0"></span>**Theorem F.12.** Any recurrent model  $\mathcal{M}$  (Definition F.10) that solves AR requires  $\max_i |\mathbf{Z}_{\mathcal{M}}^i|$  to be at least  $\Omega(N)$ -bits.

*Proof.* Consider an instance (x, i) of the index problem with  $x \in \{0, 1\}^N$ . We now describe the corresponding instance of the AR problem:

<span id="page-42-4"></span>
$$\{j, \mathbf{x}_j\}_{j=0}^{N-1}, i.$$
 (25)

<span id="page-42-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>8</sup>The randomized communication complexity of function f is defined as  $\min_{\pi} ||\pi||$ , where  $\pi$  ranges over all randomized protocols that can solve f with probability of success at least 2/3.

Next, consider the following one-way protocol for solving the index problem using the regressive model  $\mathcal{M}$ . Alice with their access of  $\boldsymbol{x} \in \{0,1\}^N$  generate an input for AR (without the query) as in equation 25. Alice then runs the model  $\mathcal{M}$  on  $\{i, \boldsymbol{x}_j\}_{j=0}^{N-1}$  and sends the memory content of running the model  $\mathcal{M}$  to Bob. This should include the state  $\boldsymbol{Z}_{\mathcal{M}}^{N-1}$  of size  $\tilde{d}$  as we can reasonably assume that both have access to the set of functions  $\{f_{\mathcal{M}}^j\}_{j\in[N]}$ . Since we assume that this model solves AR, the output  $\mathrm{Out}[N,:] = \boldsymbol{x}_i$  should contain the associated value of i. Here, Bob can compute  $\mathrm{Out}[N,:]$  by using the memory content sent by Alice and applying the function  $f^N$  as follows.

$$\boldsymbol{x}_i = \mathtt{Out}[N,:] = f^N(\boldsymbol{Z}^{N-1}, \boldsymbol{u}[N]).$$

That is, the total number of bits that are communicated in this protocol is  $|Z_{\mathcal{M}}^{N-1}|$ . Now, if  $\max_j |Z_{\mathcal{M}}^j|$  is o(N) bits, we have shown that a one-way communication protocol exists for solving the index problem exists that uses o(N) communication complexity. This contradicts Theorem F.9 and hence, we conclude that the model  $\mathcal{M}$  solving AR also needs  $\Omega(N)$  bits.  $\square$ 

<span id="page-43-1"></span>**Corollary F.13.** Given an input  $u \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$  to the AR problem, a causal Mamba model with all entries in its computation taking O(1) bits needs  $d + \overline{d} \ge \Omega(N)$  to solve AR.

*Proof.* We will first show that causal Mamba is a recurrent model. To see this, first observe equation 21 and note the fact that the input-dependent parameters  $\overline{A}$ ,  $\overline{B}$ , C,  $\Delta$  are causal as mentioned in Definition F.4.

Next, due to equation 21, in order to compute  $z_{N,:} \in \mathbb{R}^d$ , we need  $C_N \in \mathbb{R}^{\overline{d}}, \overline{B}_N \in \mathbb{R}^{\overline{d}}$  and  $\Delta_N \in \mathbb{R}^d$  along with  $h[N-1,:] \in \mathbb{R}^{\overline{d}}$ . Here, we have the (N-1)-st state  $Z_{\text{Mamba}}^{N-1} \in \mathbb{R}^{3\overline{d}+d}$  given by

$$\boldsymbol{Z}_{\texttt{Mamba}}^{N-1} := \{\boldsymbol{h}[i-1,:], \Delta_N^1, \overline{\boldsymbol{B}}_N^1, \boldsymbol{C}_N^1\},$$

where  $\Delta_N^1, \overline{B}_N^1, C_N^1$  are all linear functions of  $u[0 \cdots N-1]$  that we receive from the (N-1)-st state and we compute  $\Delta_N^2, \overline{B}_N^2, C_N^2$  as linear functions of u[N] so that we have  $\Delta_N = \Delta_N^1 + \Delta_N^1, \overline{B}_N = \overline{B}_N^1 + \overline{B}_N^2, C_N = C_N^1 + C_N^2$ . We can then define the function  $f^N$  as follows:

$$\begin{split} \boldsymbol{Z}_{\text{Mamba}}^{N}[j] &= \exp(\Delta_{N}[j]\boldsymbol{A})\boldsymbol{h}[N-1,j] + \overline{\boldsymbol{B}}_{N}\boldsymbol{u}[N,j] \\ &= \overline{\boldsymbol{A}}_{N}\boldsymbol{h}[N-1,j] + \overline{\boldsymbol{B}}_{N}\boldsymbol{u}[N,j], \\ \text{Out}[N,j] &= f^{N}(\boldsymbol{Z}_{\text{Mamba}}^{N-1})[j] = \boldsymbol{C}_{N}^{\top}\boldsymbol{Z}_{\text{Mamba}}^{N}[j]. \end{split}$$

Thus, due to Theorem F.12, we can conclude that  $|\mathbf{Z}_{\mathtt{Mamba}}^{N-1}|$  does require  $\Omega(N)$ -bits to solve AR. Finally, assuming each entry of  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathtt{Mamba}}^{N-1}$  needs O(1) bits to represent, the overall state  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathtt{Mamba}}^{N-1}$  needs  $O(d+\overline{d})$  to represent, which completes the proof of the claim.

#### F.4.3. Lower Bound on the Number of Layers for AR

Next, we will again use Theorem F.9 to provide a better bound on the number of layers required to solve AR. (Note that since AR is a special case of MQAR, the result below immediately implies Theorem 3.4.)

<span id="page-43-0"></span>**Theorem F.14.** Given an input  $u \in \{0,1\}^{N \times d}$  to the AR problem with any encoding such that  $\log c \le d \le 2^{(\log N)^{1-\epsilon}}$  for  $\epsilon > 0$ , and c possible tokens from the vocabulary with  $c \le N$ , a data-independent BaseConv model with model parameters taking  $O(\log N)$  bits needs  $\Omega(\epsilon \log \log N)$  layers to solve AR.

*Proof.* For a BaseConv model that solves AR using L layers, by definition, there exists a polynomial P(u) of degree at most  $2^L$  that solves AR for any  $u \in \{0,1\}^{N \times d9}$ . This is because for the output of the ith layer of BaseConv, given by  $Z^i_{\text{BaseConv}}$ , we have

$$\boldsymbol{Z}_{\text{BaseConv}}^{i}(\boldsymbol{Y}_{\mathcal{M}}^{i-1}) \equiv P^{i}(\boldsymbol{Z}_{\text{BaseConv}}^{i-1}), \quad \deg(P^{i}) \leq 2,$$

for some polynomial  $P^i$  of degree 2 which simply takes the inner products allowing the model to solve AR, where  $\mathbf{Z}^0_{\text{BaseConv}} := \mathbf{u}$ . Further, for such a model with L layers, by composition, the output of the i-th layer for  $i \in [L]$  is also a polynomial over the input  $\mathbf{u}$  and has degree at most  $2^i$ . At the end, we have a polynomial  $P(\mathbf{u})$  of degree  $\leq 2^L$ 

<span id="page-43-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>9</sup>Since BaseConv is data independent, note that the polynomial  $P(\cdot)$  is defined once we fix N and d.

for u ∈ {0, 1} N×d . As in the proof of Theorem [F.12,](#page-42-0) again take the instance instance (x, i) of the index problem with x ∈ {0, 1} <sup>N</sup> and the corresponding instance of the AR problem as before

<span id="page-44-0"></span>
$$\mathbf{u} := \{j, \mathbf{x}_j\}_{j=0}^{N-1}, i. \tag{26}$$

Next, we build the following one-way protocol for solving the index problem using the BaseConv model from the hypothesis that it solves AR. Alice with their access of x ∈ {0, 1} <sup>N</sup> will again generate an input u for AR (without the query) as in equation [26.](#page-44-0)

Alice first takes the values a := u[0 : N − 2, :] ∈ {0, 1} (N−1)×d and substitutes these known (N − 1)d values to define the following polynomial:

$$Q(\mathbf{u}_{N-1,0},\ldots,\mathbf{u}_{N-1,d-1}) = P(\mathbf{a},\mathbf{u}_{N-1,0},\ldots,\mathbf{u}_{N-1,d-1}).$$
(27)

Here, note that Q is a polynomial in d variables that correspond to the values u[N − 1, :] that Bob has and trivially has degree D ≤ 2 <sup>L</sup>. Now, Alice can run the model M, retrieve the coefficients of Q, and send it to Bob. Since we assume that P solves AR, Bob can take the coefficients of Q and substitute u[N − 1, :] to Q to compute P(u) which is the associated value of i.

Here, the polynomial Q that Alice sends has at most d 2 L coefficients as each term in Q can have degree at most 2 L. If each such coefficient has B bits, then using Theorem [F.9,](#page-42-2) the total number of bits being communicated must satisfy B · d 2 L ≥ Ω(N). This follows from the fact that if B · d 2 L ≤ o(N), then since the associated value of i in equation [26](#page-44-0) is the answer to the indexing problem, we have shown that a one-way communication protocol for solving the index problem uses o(N) communication complexity, which then contradicts Theorem [F.9.](#page-42-2) Thus, we must have

$$B \cdot d^{2^L} \ge \Omega(N) \implies 2^L \log(d) \ge \log\left(\frac{N}{B}\right) - O(1).$$

Taking logarithm of both sides then yields

$$L \ge \log\left(\frac{\log\left(\frac{N}{B}\right)}{\log\left(d\right)}\right) - O(1) \ge \log\left(\frac{\log N - \log B}{\log\left(d\right)}\right) - O(1)$$

$$\ge \log\left(\frac{\log N - \log B}{(\log N)^{1-\epsilon}}\right),\tag{28}$$

where we use the fact that d ≤ 2 (log N) 1−ϵ for any ϵ > 0 in equation [28.](#page-44-1)

Moreover, as the model parameters are assumed to be O(log N) bits, any coefficient in Q should have absolute value at most (︁ 2 O(log N) · N d)︁<sup>2</sup> L as each coefficient can be a product of at most N d variables. That is, for some α > 0, we have the following bound on each coefficient:

<span id="page-44-3"></span><span id="page-44-1"></span>
$$2^{B} \le (N^{\alpha+1}d)^{2^{L}} \le (N^{(\alpha+2)})^{2^{L}}$$

where the last equality uses the fact that d ≤ N. We thus have

<span id="page-44-4"></span><span id="page-44-2"></span>
$$\log(B) \le \log(\alpha + 2) + L + \log\log N. \tag{29}$$

Substituting equation [29](#page-44-2) to equation [28,](#page-44-1) we get

$$L \ge \log\left(\frac{\log N - \log(\alpha + 2) - L - \log\log N}{(\log N)^{1 - \epsilon}}\right) \tag{30}$$

Now, if L > log log N, we are done. Otherwise, if L ≤ log log N, then we can substitute this to equation [30](#page-44-3) to get

$$L \ge \log \left( \frac{\log N - \log(\alpha + 2) - 2\log\log N}{(\log N)^{1-\epsilon}} \right)$$

$$= \log (\log N - \log(\alpha + 2) - 2\log\log N) - (1-\epsilon)\log\log N$$
(31)

We now claim that first term in equation [31](#page-44-4) satisfies the following:

<span id="page-44-5"></span>
$$\log(\log N - \log(\alpha + 2) - 2\log\log N) \ge (1 - \frac{\epsilon}{2})\log\log N. \tag{32}$$

To see this, note that, for sufficiently large enough N, the following holds:

$$\frac{\log N}{2} \ge \log(\alpha + 2) + 2\log\log N,$$

hence, we get

$$\log\left(\log N - \log(\alpha + 2) - 2\log\log N\right) \ge \log\left(\frac{\log N}{2}\right) \ge \log\log N - 1 \ge (1 - \frac{\epsilon}{2})\log\log N.$$

This proves the claim in equation 32. Finally, using equation 32, equation 31 leads to the following:

$$L \geq (1 - \frac{\epsilon}{2}) \log \log N - (1 - \epsilon) \log \log N = \frac{\epsilon}{2} \log \log N,$$

which still provides the lower bound  $L = \Omega(\epsilon \log \log N)$ , as desired.

Remark F.15. We remark that it is possible to extend Theorem F.14 to any model whose output from each layer is a polynomial of some degree  $\Delta \geq 2$  ot get a lower bound of  $\Omega(\epsilon \log \log N / \log \Delta)$ .

### F.4.4. Lower Bound on the Number of Layers for MQAR with $d = \log_2 c$

**Setup.** We take  $d = \log_2 c$  to encode all c possible tokens from C. That is, all the  $2^d$  possible d-bit vectors can appear as a token in the input for MQAR. We will show that data-independent BaseConv needs  $\Omega(\log d) = \Omega(\log \log c)$ -layers to solve this setting of MQAR, while Attention (+ReLU) can solve this in O(1) layers.

We first provide the trivial solution using Attention (+ReLU).

**Proposition F.16.** Attention (with linear biases and ReLU) followed by two layers of MLPs can solve MQAR for an input sequence  $\mathbf{u} \in \{0, 1\}^{3N \times d}$  such that  $d = \log_2(c)$  in O(1) layers.

*Proof.* Given a row  $\boldsymbol{u}[i,:] \in \{0,1\}^d$ , we express each row as  $\boldsymbol{w}[i,:] \in \{-1,1\}^d$  by applying the projection  $\boldsymbol{u}\boldsymbol{W} + \boldsymbol{B}$ , where  $\boldsymbol{W} := \operatorname{diag}(2,\ldots,2) \in \mathbb{R}^{d\times d}$  and the bias matrix  $\boldsymbol{B}$  is the matrix of all -1's so that  $\boldsymbol{w}[i,j] = 2\boldsymbol{u}[i,j] - 1$ . Then, we can specify the query and key projection matrices  $\mathbf{Q}, \mathbf{K}, \mathbf{V} \in \mathbb{R}^{3N\times d}$  as follows:

$$\begin{split} \mathbf{K}[i,:] &\equiv \begin{cases} \boldsymbol{w}[i,:] = \boldsymbol{k}_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} & \text{if } i \equiv 0 \mod 3 \\ \mathbf{0} & \text{otherwise} \end{cases} \\ \mathbf{Q}[i,:] &\equiv \begin{cases} \boldsymbol{w}[i,:] = \boldsymbol{q}_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} & \text{if } i \equiv 2 \mod 3 \\ \mathbf{0} & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}, \\ \mathbf{V}[i,:] &\equiv \begin{cases} \boldsymbol{w}[i+1,:] = \boldsymbol{v}_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} & \text{if } i \equiv 0 \mod 3 \\ \mathbf{0} & \text{otherwise} \end{cases} \end{split}$$

where the values are shifted to the corresponding key index. Computing the pair-wise inner products then yields

$$\mathbf{Q}\mathbf{K}^{\top}[i,j] \equiv \begin{cases} \langle \boldsymbol{q}_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor}, \boldsymbol{k}_{\lfloor j/3 \rfloor} \rangle & \text{if } i \equiv 2 \mod 3 \text{ and } j \equiv 0 \mod 3 \\ \mathbf{0} & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}$$

However, since both  $q_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor}, k_{\lfloor j/3 \rfloor} \in \{-1,1\}^d$ , we have  $\langle q_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor}, k_{\lfloor j/3 \rfloor} \rangle \leq d$  with equality iff  $q_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} \equiv k_{\lfloor j/3 \rfloor}$ . We then subtract off d-1 from each of the  $3N \times 3N$  entries by taking the bias  $\mathbf{B} \in \mathbb{R}^{3N \times 3N}$  as the matrix with each entry -d+1. Let  $\mathbf{Z} := \mathrm{ReLU}(\mathbf{Q}\mathbf{K}^\top + \mathbf{B})$  so that we have

$$\mathbf{Z}[i,j] = \mathbb{1}\{q_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} \equiv k_{\lfloor j/3 \rfloor}\}.$$

Next, as we may have multiple matches and we only need to return 1, we modify Z by multiplying with the matrices  $W_1, W_2 \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times d}$  and adding the bias  $B \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times d}$  defined as follows:

$$\boldsymbol{W}_1[k,j] := \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } k \geq j \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}, \quad \boldsymbol{W}_2[\ell,k] := \begin{cases} -1 & \text{if } k = 0 \\ 1 & \text{if } k = \ell, \ell \neq 0 \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}.$$

For  $Z_1 := ZW_1$  and  $Z_2 := ZW_1W_2$ , we have:

$$\begin{aligned} \bm{Z}_1[i,j] &= \sum_k \bm{Z}[i,k] \bm{W}_1[k,j] = \sum_{k \geq j} \bm{Z}[i,k], \ \bm{Z}_2[i,j] &= \sum_k \bm{Z}_1[i,k] \bm{W}_2[k,j] = \bm{Z}_1[i,j] - \bm{Z}_1[i,0]. \end{aligned}$$

That is, each entry in  $\mathbb{Z}_1$  sums the entries in the row that are at the same or higher column index while each column in  $\mathbb{Z}_2$  subtracts the first entry—the sum of all entries in the row—from each entry in the row. Semantically, for each row in  $\mathbb{Z}_1$ , the entries from 0 to the index of the first match must have the same value, and thus, are the only non-negative entries in  $\mathbb{Z}_2$ . Next, we add the bias and activate under RELU to get  $\mathbb{Z}' \in \mathbb{R}^{3N \times d}$ :

$$\bm{Z}'[i,k] := \mathtt{RELU}(\bm{Z}_2 + \bm{B})[i,k] = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } k \leq \min\{j| \ \bm{q}_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} \equiv \bm{k}_{\lfloor j/3 \rfloor} \} \ 0 & \text{otherwise}. \end{cases}$$

Now, we multiply by the weight matrix  $\boldsymbol{W}_3 \in \mathbb{R}^{3N \times d}$  defined as

$$\bm{W}_3[k,j] := \begin{cases} -1 & \text{if } k=j+1 \ 1 & \text{if } k=j \ 0 & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}$$

This yields the retriever  $\overline{Z} = Z'W_3 \in \mathbb{R}^{3N \times d}$  given by

$$\overline{\boldsymbol{Z}}[i,k] := \sum_{\ell} \boldsymbol{Z}'[i,\ell] \boldsymbol{W}_{3}[\ell,k] = \boldsymbol{Z}'[i,k] - \boldsymbol{Z}'[i,k+1] = \mathbb{1}\{k = \min\{j | \boldsymbol{q}_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} \equiv \boldsymbol{k}_{\lfloor j/3 \rfloor}\}\}.$$

Finally, we multiply with the values V to get

$$(\overline{Z}\mathbf{V})[i,:] \equiv \overline{Z}[i,:]\mathbf{V} \equiv \overline{Z}[i,j^*] \cdot \mathbf{V}[j^*,:] \equiv \begin{cases} \mathbf{v}_{j^*} & \text{if } \mathbf{q}_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} \equiv \mathbf{k}_{\lfloor j^*/3 \rfloor}, j^* = \min\{j \mid \mathbf{q}_{\lfloor i/3 \rfloor} \equiv \mathbf{k}_{\lfloor j/3 \rfloor}\}. \\ \mathbf{0} & \text{if no such } j^* \text{ exists.} \end{cases}$$

That is, the row corresponding to the query returns the value associated to the first matching key. Thus, the model with Attention (computing Z) followed by two MLPs computing Z' and  $\overline{Z}$ , respectively, solves the MQAR problem.

Next, we relate the output of L layers of BaseConv to the degree of the polynomial that it computes.

<span id="page-46-1"></span>**Lemma F.17.** For any input sequence u, there exists a multilinear polynomial equivalent (over Boolean inputs) to the polynomial computed by L layers of BaseConv with degree at most  $2^L$ .

*Proof.* Let P(u) be the polynomial computed by L layers of BaseConv. Since the output of a single layer of BaseConv is equivalent to a polynomial over the input variables with degree at most 2, composing L such layers yields a polynomial of degree at most  $2^L$ . However, P(u) need not be multi linear, but the polynomial defined as

$$Q(\mathbf{u}) := (\cdots ((P(\mathbf{u}) \mod (u_1^2 - u_1)) \mod (u_2^2 - u_2)) \cdots) \mod (u_{3Nd}^2 - u_{3Nd})$$

is equivalent to  $P(\boldsymbol{u})$  as  $(u_i^2 - u_i)$  evaluates to 0 for each input var  $u_i \in \{0,1\}$ . However,  $\deg(Q(\boldsymbol{u})) \leq \deg(P(\boldsymbol{u}))$ , and thus, the claim holds.

We now relate the MQAR (in the above setting) to the degree of the polynomial that it computes.

<span id="page-46-0"></span>**Lemma F.18.** The MQAR problem with  $d = \log_2(c)$  is represented by a multi-linear polynomial of degree 2d + 1.

*Proof.* We will start by specifying the obvious Boolean circuit that solves MQAR. First, we take the XNOR of keys and queries bitwise as follows.

$$x^{ij} = q_i \operatorname{xnor} k_j := (q_i \wedge k_j) \vee (\neg q_i \wedge \neg k_j) \text{ for } i > j,$$
(33)

where, for x, y ∈ {0, 1} d , we have

$$[\boldsymbol{x} \; \text{xnor} \; \boldsymbol{y}][k] := \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if} \; \boldsymbol{x}[k] = \boldsymbol{y}[k] \\ 0 & \text{othwerise} \end{cases}$$

That is, each bit from x ij is set to 1 iff the corresponding bits from q<sup>i</sup> and k<sup>j</sup> match. Next, we take the AND of the d-bits to get

<span id="page-47-1"></span>
$$\mathbf{y}^{ij} := \bigwedge_{k \in [d]} \mathbf{x}_k^{ij}, i > j. \tag{34}$$

Thus, y ij is set to 1 iff the query q<sup>i</sup> matches with the key k<sup>j</sup> . Finally, we AND with each bit of the values to get the output z ij with the kth bit for k ∈ [d] given by

$$\boldsymbol{z}_k^{ij} := \boldsymbol{y}_{ij} \wedge [\boldsymbol{v}_j]_k. \tag{35}$$

Thus, the output of the circuit can be represented as

$$\bm{z}^{ij} = \begin{cases} \bm{v}_i & \text{if } \bm{q}_i \equiv \bm{k}_j, i > j \\ \bm{0} & \text{otherwise}. \end{cases}$$

We can now directly translate the above circuit into a multi-linear polynomial. With slight abuse of notation, we have the following correspondence for equation [34,](#page-47-1) where u<sup>i</sup> ≡ q<sup>i</sup> ,u<sup>j</sup> ≡ k<sup>j</sup> , i > j and we use uij to represent the variable corresponding to the entry u[i, j].

$$\boldsymbol{x}_k^{ij}(\boldsymbol{u}) := \boldsymbol{u}_{ik} \boldsymbol{u}_{jk} + (1 - \boldsymbol{u}_{ik})(1 - \boldsymbol{u}_{jk}) \quad \text{for each } k \in [d], i > j.$$

Next, we translate equation [34](#page-47-1) as follows.

$$y^{ij}(u) := \prod_{k \in [d]} (u_{ik}u_{jk} + (1 - u_{ik})(1 - u_{jk})).$$

Finally, we can write the polynomial that computes MQAR as follows.

<span id="page-47-2"></span>
$$\boldsymbol{z}^{ij}(\boldsymbol{u}) := \left(\prod_{k \in [d]} \boldsymbol{u}_{ik} \boldsymbol{u}_{jk} + (1 - \boldsymbol{u}_{ik})(1 - \boldsymbol{u}_{jk})\right) \boldsymbol{u}_{(i+1)k} \quad \text{for each } k \in [d], i > j, \tag{36}$$

where u[i + 1, :] ≡ v<sup>j</sup> . It is then easy to observe that equation [36](#page-47-2) is multi-linear and has degree 2d + 1.

We are now ready to provide the lower bound.

<span id="page-47-0"></span>Theorem F.19. *A data-independent* BaseConv *model needs* log(2d)*-layers to solve* MQAR *for an input sequence* u ∈ {0, 1} <sup>3</sup>N×<sup>d</sup> *with* d = log<sup>2</sup> (c)*.*

*Proof.* Due to Lemma [F.18,](#page-46-0) we know there exists a multi-linear polynomial that solves MQAR, and due to [\(Kopparty,](#page-13-3) [2020,](#page-13-3) Lecture 3, Proposition 4), it is unique. Specifically we cannot solve MQAR with a multi-linear polynomial of degree ≤ 2d. Now, assume that there is a BaseConv model with L layers that exactly solves MQAR. Then, due to Lemma [F.17,](#page-46-1) this yields a multilinear polynomial P(u) of degree at most 2 <sup>L</sup>. Here, if L ≤ log(2d), then the resulting BaseConv with L layers results in a multilinear polynomial of degree ≤ 2d. This contradicts the above claim that we cannot have a multi linear polynomial of degree < 2d + 1 that exactly represents MQAR. Consequently, a data-independent BaseConv model needs ≥ log(2d)-layers to solve MQAR.

#### F.5. Lower Bound on the Number of Layers for d ≥ log<sup>2</sup> c with Specific Encodings

### F.5.1. THE EQUALITY PROBLEM

For an input pair u1,u<sup>2</sup> where each u<sup>i</sup> is a token drawn from a vocabulary of size c = |C| and embedded in {0, 1} d , we define the *equality problem* (EQ) as checking whether the two encodings are equal: u<sup>1</sup> ≡ u2.

<span id="page-47-3"></span>We first note that any model that solves MQAR also solves EQ via the following proposition.

**Proposition F.20.** Any model  $M_{MQAR}$  that solves MQAR also solves EQ using the same number of layers.

*Proof.* If there exists a model  $M_{MQAR}$  that solves MQAR using L layers, then for an arbitrary input instance for EQ given by  $u_1, u_2 \in \mathbb{R}^{2 \times d}$ , we can produce the following input instance for MQAR:  $u := \{(u_1, 1, u_1), (u_2, 1, u_2)\}$  and solve EQ using L layers with  $M_{MQAR}$  returning 1 iff there is a match.

Due to Proposition F.20, we obtain the following corollary.

<span id="page-48-4"></span>**Corollary F.21.** Any lower bound  $\overline{L}$  on the number of layers L of BaseConv to solving EQ is also a lower bound on the number of layers required for solving MQAR.

We now try to prove a lower bound for the case of  $d \ge \log_2 c$ . First, note that there are embeddings here where the lower bound from F.19 holds: consider the embedding where the first  $\log_2 c$  has the compact binary embedding as before but the last  $d - \log_2 c$  bits are the same for all the tokens. We will instead prove a lower bound for a more interesting set of embeddings.

### F.5.2. The *p*-Hot Encoding for $p \ge 1$

<span id="page-48-0"></span>**Definition F.22** ((Almost) p-Hot Encoding). We define the p-hot encoding to be the collection of embeddings for a token  $x_t$  with  $0 \le t < c$  such that we express t in base  $\sqrt[p]{c}: (t_0,...,t_{p-1}) \in [0,\sqrt[p]{c})^p$  and represent each  $t_i$  as one hot encoding in  $\{0,1\}$   $\sqrt[p]{c}$ . That is, we take  $d=p\cdot\sqrt[p]{c}$ .

Moreover, we define the *almost p-hot encoding* to be the collection of embeddings where each  $t_i$  is mapped in  $\{0,1\}^{\sqrt[p]{c}-1}$  obtained by dropping the last bit of its one-hot encoding in  $\{0,1\}^{\sqrt[p]{c}}$ .

Note that both of the encodings have p-many blocks derived from each of the one-hot encodings.

<span id="page-48-1"></span>**Definition F.23** (Block-Exclusive). We say that a polynomial P with variables in  $u := (u_0, \dots, u_{p-1})$  is block-exclusive if each non-zero monomial in P given by the product

$$\prod_{i \in [p], \ j \in [\sqrt[p]{c}]} u_{i,j}$$

does not contain any product of the form  $u_{i,j}u_{i,j'}$  for  $i \in [p], j, j' \in [\sqrt[p]{c}]$ .

Remark F.24. The condition specified in Definition F.23 ensures that a block-exclusive polynomial is necessarily multilinear, as it disallows the term  $u_{i,j}u_{i,j'}$  for j=j' in any non-zero monomial.

<span id="page-48-2"></span>**Lemma F.25.** For any Boolean function  $f: \{0,1\} \to \{0,1\}$  with inputs from the almost p-hot encoding or the p-hot encoding setting, there exists a block-exclusive polynomial equivalent to f.

*Proof.* Given an input  $\boldsymbol{u}$  to f from the almost p-hot encoding or the p-hot encoding such that  $\boldsymbol{u} := (\boldsymbol{u}_0, \dots, \boldsymbol{u}_{p-1})$ , we first observe that the polynomial  $P(\boldsymbol{u})$  representing  $f(\boldsymbol{u})$  cannot have a non-zero monomial with variables from the same block. Specifically, for  $0 \le j < p$ , any non-zero monomial in P cannot have a product of the form  $\boldsymbol{u}_{j,k}\boldsymbol{u}_{j,k'}$  for  $k \ne k'$ . To see this, assume that there exists a non-zero monomial in P with at least two terms  $\boldsymbol{u}_{j,k}\boldsymbol{u}_{j,k'}$  from the same jth block in  $\boldsymbol{u}$ , then monomial always evaluates to 0 as the jth block is derived from the one-hot encoding in  $\{0,1\}^{\sqrt[p]{c}}$  or the almost one-hot encoding in  $\{0,1\}^{\sqrt[p]{c}-1}$ , and hence, cannot have more than one bit set to 1.

Next, if a non-zero monomial in P does contain a product of the form  $u_{j,k}u_{j,k'}$  for  $k,k' \in [\sqrt[p]{c}]$ , we can define the polynomial

$$Q(\boldsymbol{u}) := (\cdots ((P(\boldsymbol{u}) \mod (u_{0,0}^2 - u_{0,0})) \mod (u_{0,1}^2 - u_{0,1})) \cdots) \mod (u_{p-1,\sqrt[p]{c}-1}^2 - u_{p-1,\sqrt[p]{c}-1}).$$

Since each entry is Boolean, Q is equivalent to P over Boolean inputs, and thus, Q is the block-exclusive polynomial equivalent to f.

<span id="page-48-3"></span>**Proposition F.26.** Any Boolean function  $f: \{0,1\} \to \{0,1\}$  with inputs from the almost p-hot encoding setting has a unique representation as a block-exclusive polynomial.

*Proof.* Due to (Kopparty, 2020, Proposition 4), we know that every Boolean function f is represented by a multilinear polynomial. Moreover, from Lemma F.25, we know that the polynomial P(u) representing f(u) is block-exclusive for u with the almost p-hot encoding.

To show uniqueness, we replicate the argument from (Kopparty, 2020, Lecture 3, Proposition 4): Given two block-exclusive polynomials P and P' equivalent to f with inputs from the almost p-hot encoding, we have  $(P-P')(u) \equiv 0$ . Now, assume, for the sake of contradiction, that  $P-P'\not\equiv 0$ . Here, note that as P-P' is not identically zero and we have a non-zero monomial, and since the inputs are from the almost p-hot encoding, we know that this monomial cannot contain any product of the form  $u_{j,k}u_{j,k'}$ . Let  $S\subseteq [p]\times [\sqrt[p]{c}-1]$  be a minimal set of indices such that the monomial  $\prod_{(j,k)\in S}u_{j,k}$  appears in P-P' with non-zero coefficient. Note that  $\chi_S$  forms a valid input to f as each block in S can be assigned at most one non-zero entry. Then, since  $(P-P')(\chi_S)\neq 0$  as every other monomial will get at least one variable that is assigned to 0 for  $\chi_S$ , we achieve a contradiction, and thus, P-P' must be identically zero on inputs from the almost p-hot encoding.  $\square$ 

<span id="page-49-1"></span>**Lemma F.27.** The EQ problem in the almost p-hot encoding setting is represented by a block-exclusive polynomial of degree 2p.

*Proof.* Each input pair  $u^1, u^2$  to the EQ problem can be represented as  $u^i := (u^i_0, \dots, u^i_{p-1})$  for  $i \in \{1, 2\}$ , where for each 0 < j < p such that we have

$$\bm{u}_{j}^{i} := (\bm{u}_{j,0}^{i}, \dots, \bm{u}_{j, \sqrt[p]{c}-2}^{i}) \in \{0,1\}^{\sqrt[p]{c}-1}.$$

The following polynomial takes the inner product of each of these one-hot encodings:

$$P^{j}(\boldsymbol{u}) := \sum_{k=0}^{\sqrt[p]{c}-2} \boldsymbol{u}_{j,k}^{1} \cdot \boldsymbol{u}_{j,k}^{2} + (1 - \sum_{k=0}^{\sqrt[p]{c}-2} \boldsymbol{u}_{j,k}^{1})(1 - \sum_{k=0}^{\sqrt[p]{c}-2} \boldsymbol{u}_{j,k}^{2})$$

for 0 < j < p. Here, note that there can be only be at most 1 in both  $u_j^1$  and  $u_j^2$ , and thus,  $P^j(u) = 1$  iff the jth block agree. Next, the following polynomial is equivalent to the Boolean function that solves the EQ problem:

$$P(\boldsymbol{u}) := \prod_{j=0}^{p-1} P^j(\boldsymbol{u}),$$

and we have  $P(u) = \mathbb{1}\{u^1 \equiv u^2\}$ . Here, note that P is multi-linear and has degree 2p as each  $P^j$  is a degree-2 polynomial. Moreover, P is block-exclusive as each  $P^j$  is block-exclusive and we only multiply monomials from different blocks in P.

<span id="page-49-2"></span>**Proposition F.28.** Let P be the block-exclusive polynomial that solves the EQ problem in the p-hot encoding. Then,  $deg(P) \ge 2p$ .

*Proof.* For the sake of contradiction, assume that there exists a block-exclusive polynomial P that solves EQ in the p-hot encoding setting with degree  $\leq 2p-1$ . Then, given an input  $\boldsymbol{u}:=(\boldsymbol{u}_0,\ldots,\boldsymbol{u}_{p-1})$  from the almost p-hot encoding, where each block  $\boldsymbol{u}_i$  corresponds to the truncated bit string from the one-hot encoding in  $\{0,1\}^{\sqrt[p]{c}-1}$ , we can convert this input to the p-hot encoding  $\boldsymbol{v}:=(\boldsymbol{v}_0,\ldots,\boldsymbol{v}_{p-1})$  as follows:

$$\boldsymbol{v}_i := \left(\boldsymbol{u}_{i,0}, \ldots, \boldsymbol{u}_{i,\sqrt[p]{c}-2}, 1 - \sum_{j=0}^{\sqrt[p]{c}-2} \boldsymbol{u}_{i,j}\right)$$

Then, the block-wise multilinear polynomial Q(u) = P(v) solves the EQ problem in the almost one-hot encoding setting and has  $\deg(Q) \leq \deg(P) \leq 2p-1$  which contradicts the combination of Proposition F.26 and Lemma F.27.

<span id="page-49-0"></span>**Theorem F.29.** A data-independent BaseConv model needs at least  $\lfloor \log(2p) \rfloor$ -layers to solve MQAR for an input sequence  $\mathbf{u} \in \{0,1\}^{3N \times d}$  in the p-hot encoding setting, where  $d = p \cdot \sqrt[p]{c}$ .

*Proof.* We know from Corollary F.21 that it suffices to show a lower bound for the EQ problem. Moreover, we know from Proposition F.28 that we cannot solve the EQ problem in the p-hot encoding setting with a block-exclusive polynomial of degree  $\leq 2p-1$ . Now, assume that there is a BaseConv model with L layers that exactly solves EQ in the p-hot encoding setting. Then, due to Lemma F.17 and Proposition F.26, this yields a block-exclusive polynomial P(u) of degree at most  $2^L$ . Here, if  $L < |\log(2p)|$  which, then the resulting BaseConv with L layers results in a block-exclusive polynomial of degree  $\leq 2p-1$ . This contradicts the above claim that we cannot have a block-exclusive polynomial of degree  $\leq 2p$  that exactly represents EQ. Consequently, a data-independent BaseConv model needs  $> |\log(2p)|$ -layers to solve EQ.

### F.6. Upperbound on MQAR with sub-logarithmically many BaseConv layers

**Setup:** For an input  $Q, K, V \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$ , the MQAR problem is computing  $(C \odot (QK^{\top})) \times V$  where  $C \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times N}$  is a lower triangular matrix with 1s in all possible non-zero positions:

$$C[i,j] \equiv \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } j \leq i \\ 0 & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

Further, we define the following notation:

$$b = \lceil \log(N+1) \rceil,$$
  
$$\bar{b} = \lceil \log(d+1) \rceil,$$
  
$$\bar{N} = \max(N, d).$$

Finally, we define  $bin(i) \in \{0,1\}^b$  to be the binary representation of  $1 \le i \le N$  and  $bin(j) \in \{0,1\}^{\overline{b}}$  to be the binary representation of  $1 \le j \le d$ . All vectors are assumed to be in column form and all row and column indices will start from 1.

We assume that:

- (i) Each row of Q and K use 1-hot encoding and
- (ii) Each query matches with at most one key.

We show that BaseConv can compute the MQAR problem with  $O(\log \log(\overline{N}))$  layers:

<span id="page-50-0"></span>**Theorem F.30.** The MQAR problem with 1-hot encoded tokens, at most one key match per query, and  $\overline{N} \geq 8$  can be solved with  $BaseConv(N, O(\log\log(\overline{N})), d, O(\overline{N}\log(\overline{N})), O(\overline{N}\log(\overline{N})))$ .

#### <span id="page-50-1"></span>F.6.1. BASECONV PRIMITIVES

In this section we show some basic primitives that will be helpful in proving Theorem F.30.

We define  $e_i^{(j)}$  to be the the *i*th standard basis vector with a dimension of *j* (remember, both are one based indexing).

We first define the primitives and then show how to implement them using BaseConv.

Note that if a convolution matrix, denoted with h are given as a single column, all columns of this matrix are identical as as defined. Specifically, if given  $h \in \mathbb{R}^N$  and  $x \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$  we have h \* h to denote k \* x where  $k[:,j] = h \forall j \in [d]$ .

 $\begin{array}{l} \textbf{Definition F.31. repeat\_columns}(\boldsymbol{y},r) \\ \textbf{INPUT: } \boldsymbol{y} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'r}, r \in \mathbb{Z}^+. \end{array}$ 

OUTPUT:  $z \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'r}$ , where z has each of the first d' columns of y repeated r times. In other words,

$$\bm{y} \equiv \left(\bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow 1}, \dots, \bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow d'}, \stackrel{\uparrow}{\downarrow}, \dots, \stackrel{\uparrow}{\downarrow} \right), \bm{z} \equiv \left(\bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow 1}, \dots \bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow 1}, \dots, \bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow d'}, \dots, \bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow d'}, \dots, \bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow d'} \right).$$

**Definition F.32.** repeat\_matrix(y,r) INPUT:  $y \in \mathbb{R}^{N'r \times d'}, r \in \mathbb{Z}^+$  such that  $y[N'+1:,:] = \mathbf{0}^{N'(r-1) \times d'}$ .

OUTPUT:  $z \in \mathbb{R}^{N'r \times d'}$ , where z is the first N' rows of y repeated r times. In other words,

$$\begin{aligned} \boldsymbol{y} \equiv \begin{pmatrix} \leftarrow \boldsymbol{y}[1:N',:] \rightarrow \ \boldsymbol{0}^{N' \times d'} \end{pmatrix}, \boldsymbol{z} \equiv \begin{pmatrix} \leftarrow \boldsymbol{y}[1:N',:] \rightarrow \ \leftarrow \boldsymbol{y}[1:N',:] \rightarrow \ & \vdots \ & \vdots \ \leftarrow \boldsymbol{y}[1:N',:] \rightarrow \end{pmatrix} r \text{ times} \end{aligned}$$

## **Definition F.33.** cumulative\_sum(y)

Input:  $\boldsymbol{y} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$ .

OUTPUT:  $z \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$ , where each row of z is the sum of all rows of y with a smaller or equal index. In other words,

$$\begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \biglet \begin{aligned} \biglet \begin{aligned} \biglet \begin{aligned} \biglet \begin{aligned} \biglet \begin{aligned} \biglet \begin{aligned} ## **Definition F.34.** sum\_all\_columns(y)

Input:  $\boldsymbol{y} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$ . OUTPUT:  $\boldsymbol{z} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$ 

where the first column of z has the sum of all columns of y and the rest are all zeros. In other words,

$$\bm{y} \equiv \left(\bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow 1}, \bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow 2}, \ldots, \bm{y}_{\downarrow}^{(d')}\right), \bm{z} \equiv \left(\sum_{j=1}^{d'} \bm{y}^{(j)}, \bm{0}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow}, \ldots, \bm{0}_{\downarrow}^{\uparrow}\right).$$

## **Definition F.35.** sum\_column\_blocks( $\boldsymbol{y}, B$ )

INPUT:  $\boldsymbol{y} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$ ,  $B \in \mathbb{Z}$  such that B divides d'.

OUTPUT:  $\boldsymbol{z} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$ , where of column blocks the first column block of  $\boldsymbol{z}$  is the sum of all column blocks and the rest are zero. In other words,

$$z[:, 1:B] \equiv \sum_{j=0}^{\frac{d'}{B}-1} y[:, jB+1:(j+1)B]$$

$$z[:,j] = \mathbf{0}^{N'}$$
 for all  $j > B$ .

Note that  $sum_all_columns(y) = sum_column_blocks(y, 1)$ .

**Definition F.36.** one\_hot\_encoding(y, d)

Input:  $\boldsymbol{y} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times N'}$ .

OUTPUT:  $z \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times N'}$ , where the first  $\lceil \log(N') \rceil$  columns of each row of y represent a binary encoding  $\overline{y}_i \in [1, N']$  which is converted to a 1-hot encoding. In other words,

$$\begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} egin{aligned\\ \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} \begin{aligned} **Definition F.37.** remember  $(\boldsymbol{y},r,t,f)$ INPUT:  $\boldsymbol{y} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}, r \in \mathbb{Z}, t \in \mathbb{Z}, f : \mathbb{R}^{t-r} \to \mathbb{R}^{t-r+s}, \boldsymbol{v}_1 \in \mathbb{R}^r, \boldsymbol{x} \in \mathbb{R}^{t-r}$ , where  $\boldsymbol{y}$  is defined as below. OUTPUT:  $\boldsymbol{z} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$ , which is defined as follows:

$$\begin{aligned} \begin{pmatrix} \leftarrow \boldsymbol{v}_1 \rightarrow \ \leftarrow \boldsymbol{x} \rightarrow \ \hline \boldsymbol{o}^{s \times d'} \ \leftarrow \boldsymbol{v}_2 \rightarrow \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \ \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \ \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \ \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \ \boldsymbol{o} \ \hline \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \$$

Recall, that shift-down(y,s) and shift-up(y,s) will shift the matrix y down or up by s rows, respectively.

<span id="page-52-0"></span>**Proposition F.38** ( (Arora et al., 2023a)). For any  $y \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times d}$ , there exist (N, 1, d, N, d) – BaseConv and (N, 3, d, N, d) – BaseConv that computes  $shift\_down(y, s)$  and  $shift\_up(y, s)$  for any  $s \le N$ .

Now we will show how each primitive is implemented in terms of BASECONV layers.

**Proposition F.39** (The Repeat Columns primitive). For any  $y \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'r}$  and any  $r \in \mathbb{Z}^+$  there exists a (N', 1, d'r, N', d'r) - BaseConv that computes repeat\_columns(y, r).

Proof. Define

$$\boldsymbol{z} \leftarrow \text{BaseConv}(\boldsymbol{y}, \boldsymbol{W}, \boldsymbol{0}^{N' \times d'}, \boldsymbol{0}^{N' \times d'}, \boldsymbol{1}^{N' \times d'}),$$

where  $\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{R}^{d' \times d'}$  is defined as:

$$W[i,j] = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } \left\lceil \frac{i}{r} \right\rceil = j \\ 0 & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

Then note that the output of this layer is:

$$\boldsymbol{z} = (\boldsymbol{y}\boldsymbol{W} + \boldsymbol{0}^{N' \times d'}) \odot (\boldsymbol{0}^{N' \times d'} * \boldsymbol{y} + \boldsymbol{1}^{N' \times d'}) = \boldsymbol{y}\boldsymbol{W} = \left(\boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{y}^{\uparrow_1}, \dots, **Proposition F.40** (The Repeat Matrix primitive). For any  $\mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{R}^{N'r \times d'}$  and any  $r \in \mathbb{Z}^+$  there exists a (N'r, 1, d', N'r, d') – BaseConv that computes repeat\_matrix $(\mathbf{y}, r)$ .

Proof. Define

$$z \leftarrow \text{BASECONV}(y, \mathbf{0}^{d' \times d'}, \mathbf{1}^{N'r \times d'}, h, \mathbf{0}^{N'r \times d'}),$$

where  $\boldsymbol{h} \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$  is defined as:

$$\boldsymbol{h}(X) \equiv \sum_{j=0}^{r-1} \boldsymbol{x}^{N'j}.$$

The computation of the convolution will result in:

$$\begin{split} (\boldsymbol{h} * \boldsymbol{y}) &= \operatorname{coeff}((\boldsymbol{y}(X)) \cdot (1 + \boldsymbol{X}^{N'} + \ldots + \boldsymbol{X}^{N' \times (r-1)}) \\ &= \operatorname{coeff}(\boldsymbol{y}(X) + \boldsymbol{y}(X) \cdot \boldsymbol{X}^{N'} + \ldots + \boldsymbol{y}(X) \cdot (\boldsymbol{X}^{N' \times (r-1)})) \\ &= \boldsymbol{y} + \operatorname{shift-down}(\boldsymbol{y}, N') + \ldots + \operatorname{shift-down}(\boldsymbol{y}, rN'). \end{split}$$

In the above, the final equality follows from Proposition F.38. The output of this layer will compute:

$$\boldsymbol{z} = (\boldsymbol{y} \cdot \boldsymbol{0}^{d' \times d'} + \boldsymbol{1}^{N' \times d'}) \odot (\boldsymbol{h} * \boldsymbol{y} + \boldsymbol{0}^{N' \times d'}) = \boldsymbol{h} * \boldsymbol{y} = \begin{pmatrix} \boldsymbol{y}[1:N',:] \ \hline \vdots \ \boldsymbol{y}[1:N',:] \end{pmatrix}.$$

In the above, the last equality follows from Proposition F.38 and the fact that  $\mathbf{y}[N'+1:,:] = \mathbf{0}^{((N'-1)\times r)\times d')}$ **Proposition F.41** (The Cumulative Sum primitive). For any  $\mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{R}^{N'\times d'}$  there exists a (N',1,d',N',d') – BaseConv that computes cumulative\_sum $(\mathbf{y})$ .

Proof. Define

$$\boldsymbol{z} \leftarrow \text{BaseConv}(\boldsymbol{y}, \boldsymbol{0}^{d' \times d'}, \boldsymbol{1}^{N' \times d'}, \boldsymbol{h}, \boldsymbol{0}^{N' \times d'}),$$

where  $h \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$  is defined as:

$$\boldsymbol{h}(X) \equiv \sum_{j=0}^{N'-1} \boldsymbol{x}^j.$$

The computation of the convolution will result in:

$$\begin{split} (\boldsymbol{h} * \boldsymbol{y}) &= \operatorname{coeff}((\boldsymbol{y}(X)) \cdot (1 + X + \ldots + X^{N'-1}) \\ &= \operatorname{coeff}(\boldsymbol{y}(X) + \boldsymbol{y}(X) \cdot X + \ldots + \boldsymbol{y}(X) \cdot X^{N'-1}) \\ &= \boldsymbol{y} + \operatorname{shift-down}(\boldsymbol{y}, 1) + \ldots + \operatorname{shift-down}(\boldsymbol{y}, N'-1). \end{split}$$

The output of this layer is:

$$\begin{pmatrix} \leftarrow \boldsymbol{y}_1 \rightarrow \ \vdots \ \leftarrow \sum_{j=1}^i \boldsymbol{y}_j \rightarrow \ \vdots \ \leftarrow \sum_{j=1}^N \boldsymbol{y}_j \rightarrow \end{pmatrix}.$$

**Proposition F.42** (The Sum All Columns primitive). For any  $y \in \mathbb{R}^{N' \times d'}$  there exists a (N', 1, d', N', d') — BaseConv that computes sum\_all\_columns(y).

Proof. Define

$$z \leftarrow \text{BASECONV}(y, W, \mathbf{0}^{N' \times d'}, \mathbf{0}^{N' \times d'}, \mathbf{1}^{N' \times d'}),$$

where  $\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{R}^{d' \times d'}$  is defined as:

$$\boldsymbol{W}[i,j] \equiv \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } j=1 \\ 0 & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

The output of this layer will be:

$$(\bm{y}\bm{W} + \bm{0}^{N' \times d'}) \odot (\bm{0}^{N' \times d'} * \bm{y} + \bm{1}^{N' \times d'}) = \bm{y}\bm{W} = \left(\sum_{j=1}^{d'} \bm{y}^{(j)}, \bm{0}, \dots, \bm{0}, \dots, \bm{0},
